<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Draft genome sequence of subterranean clover, a reference for genus Trifolium"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2016 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-08-22"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Clovers (genus Trifolium) are widely cultivated across the world as forage legumes and make a large contribution to livestock feed production and soil improvement. Subterranean clover (T. subterraneum L.) is well suited for genomic and genetic studies as a reference species in the Trifolium genus, because it is an annual with a simple genome structure (autogamous and diploid), unlike the other economically important perennial forage clovers, red clover (T. pratense) and white clover (T. repens). This report represents the first draft genome sequence of subterranean clover. The 471.8&#8201;Mb assembled sequence covers 85.4% of the subterranean clover genome and contains 42,706 genes. Eight pseudomolecules of 401.1&#8201;Mb in length were constructed, based on a linkage map consisting of 35,341 SNPs. The comparative genomic analysis revealed that different clover chromosomes showed different degrees of conservation with other Papilionoideae species. These results provide a reference for genetic and genomic analyses in the genus Trifolium and new insights into evolutionary divergence in Papilionoideae species."/>

                                        itemprop="title">article</span></li>
                </ol>
            </div>
        </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Clovers (genus <i>Trifolium</i>) are widely cultivated across the world as forage legumes and make a large contribution to livestock feed production and soil improvement. Subterranean clover (<i>T. subterraneum</i> L.) is well suited for genomic and genetic studies as a reference species in the <i>Trifolium</i> genus, because it is an annual with a simple genome structure (autogamous and diploid), unlike the other economically important perennial forage clovers, red clover (<i>T. pratense</i>) and white clover (<i>T. repens</i>). This report represents the first draft genome sequence of subterranean clover. The 471.8 Mb assembled sequence covers 85.4% of the subterranean clover genome and contains 42,706 genes. Eight pseudomolecules of 401.1 Mb in length were constructed, based on a linkage map consisting of 35,341 SNPs. The comparative genomic analysis revealed that different clover chromosomes showed different degrees of conservation with other Papilionoideae species. These results provide a reference for genetic and genomic analyses in the genus <i>Trifolium</i> and new insights into evolutionary divergence in Papilionoideae species.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The legume family (Leguminosae) is the third-largest plant family, consisting of 751 genera and ca. 19,500 species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="McGuire, W. S. Subterranean clover: Clover science and technology 515–534 (Agronomy Monograph No. 25. American Society of Agronomy, Crop Science Society of America and Soil Science Society of America 1985)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d37551e590">1</a></sup>, including several agronomically important crop and forage species. Among the forage legumes, alfalfa (<i>Medicago sativa</i> L.), white clover (<i>Trifolium repens</i> L.) and red clover (<i>T. pratense</i> L.) are the most economically important perennial pasture legumes and used in many temperate regions of the world. Of the annual forage legume species, subterranean clover (<i>T. subterraneum</i> L.) makes the greatest contribution globally to livestock feed production and soil improvement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="McGuire, W. S. Subterranean clover: Clover science and technology 515–534 (Agronomy Monograph No. 25. American Society of Agronomy, Crop Science Society of America and Soil Science Society of America 1985)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d37551e607">1</a></sup>, particularly in Australia, where it has been sown over 29 million ha and 45 cultivars have been registered since the early 1900s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Nichols, P. G. H. et al. Genetic improvement of subterranean clover (Trifolium subterraneum L.). 1. Germplasm, traits and future prospects. Crop Pasture Sci. 64, 312–346 (2013)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d37551e611">2</a></sup>. A further 11 annual clover species have been commercialized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Nichols, P. G. H. et al. Temperate pasture legumes in Australia - Their history, current use and future prospects. Crop Pasture Sci. 63, 691–725 (2012)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d37551e615">3</a></sup>.</p><p>Draft genome sequences are available in several legume species, including the crops soybean (<i>Glycine max</i> (L.) Merr.)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d37551e625">4</a></sup>, common bean (<i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i> L.)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Schmutz, J. et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat. Genet. 46, 707–713 (2014)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37551e632">5</a></sup>, chickpea (<i>Cicer arietinum</i> L)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat. Biotech. 31, 240–246 (2013)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d37551e640">6</a></sup> pigeon pea (<i>Cajanus cajan</i> (L.) Millsp.)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotech. 30, 83–89 (2012)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d37551e647">7</a></sup> and mungbean (<i>Vigna radiata</i> (L.) Wilezek)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Kang et al. Genome sequence of mungbean and insights into evolution within Vigna species. Nat. Commun. 5, 5443 (2014)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d37551e654">8</a></sup>, while among the forage legumes, draft sequences are available for red clover (<i>T. pratense</i> L.)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="De Vega, J. J. et al. Red clover (Trifolium pratense L.) draft genome provides a platform for trait improvement. Sci. Rep. 5, 17394 (2015)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37551e662">9</a></sup> and the model legume species, <i>Lotus japonicus</i> L.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome Structure of the Legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227–239 (2008)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37551e669">10</a></sup> and <i>Medicago truncatula</i> Gaertn. (barrel medic)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37551e676">11</a></sup>. No draft genome sequences have been published for <i>M. sativa</i>, <i>T. repens</i> or any of the annual <i>Trifolium</i> species.</p><p>Subterranean clover has been proposed as a reference species for genetic and genomic studies within the genus <i>Trifolium</i>, as it is an annual, diploid (2n = 16), predominantly autogamous species with a relatively small genome size of 540 Mbp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Nichols, P. G. H. et al. Genetic improvement of subterranean clover (Trifolium subterraneum L.). 1. Germplasm, traits and future prospects. Crop Pasture Sci. 64, 312–346 (2013)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d37551e696">2</a></sup> This contrasts with <i>T. pratense</i> and <i>T. repens</i>, which are allogamous perennials, with the latter also an allotetraploid, making genetic and genomic analyses difficult. Understanding the genetics of important traits in subterranean clover can provide a pathway to understanding the genetics in these more genetically complex species. The results from this study will provide a reference for genetic and genomic analyses in the <i>Trifolium</i> genus and also other forage and crop legumes.</p><p>This report presents the first draft genome sequence of an annual clover, subterranean clover. Eight pseudomolecules were constructed based on Illumina and Roche 454 assembled genome sequences and a high density SNP linkage map. For a detailed understanding of diversification among legume species, genome structures and gene characteristics were compared with four legume species in the subfamily Papilionoideae, common bean (<i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i>, v1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Schmutz, J. et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat. Genet. 46, 707–713 (2014)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37551e715">5</a></sup> <i>L. japonicus,</i> (rel.3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome Structure of the Legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227–239 (2008)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37551e722">10</a></sup><i>, M. truncatula</i> (4.0 v1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37551e728">11</a></sup> and red clover (v2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="De Vega, J. J. et al. Red clover (Trifolium pratense L.) draft genome provides a platform for trait improvement. Sci. Rep. 5, 17394 (2015)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37551e733">9</a></sup>. Common bean is classified into the Millettioid clade (warm season legumes), whereas the other three species are in the Hologalegina clade (cool season legumes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Bruneau, A. et al. Legume phylogeny and classification in the 21st century: progress, prospects and lessons for other species-rich clades. Taxon 62, 217–248 (2013)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d37551e737">12</a></sup>. <i>M. trunactula</i> and red clover belong to the same tribe, Trifolieae. Availability of a fully sequenced genome will be exploited for the analysis of genetic diversity and trait-dissection, as well as gene tagging for marker-assisted selection. Additionally, functional genomics, transcriptomics and proteomics can be used more precisely and effectively in forage legume improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and Assembly</h3><p>The Australian subterranean clover variety, cv. Daliak, was subjected to whole genome shotgun sequencing. Single-end (SE), 520–660 bp paired-end (PE) and 2 kb, 5 kb, 8 kb, 10 kb, 15 kb and 20 kb mate-pair (MP) libraries were constructed for Roche 454 GS FLX+, Illumina MiSeq and HiSeq 2000 platforms, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S1</a>). A total of 6.8 Gb overlap fragment (OF) reads were created from 16.7 Gb MiSeq PE reads by COPE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Liu, B. et al. COPE: an accurate k-mer-based pair-end reads connection tool to facilitate genome assembly. Bioinformatics 28, 2870–2874 (2012)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d37551e759">13</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S1</a>). Together with the 2.8 Gb 454 reads, the 6.8 Gb OF reads were assembled by Newbler 2.7. The resultant number of contigs was 101,010, totaling 414.8 Mb in length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S2</a>). In parallel, 23.2 Gb HiSeq SE and PE reads were assembled by SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d37551e769">14</a></sup> (kmer = 61) and GapCloser 1.10 (<i>p</i> = 31). The assembled 603,937 sequences, totaling 421.8 Mb in length, were merged with the 101,010 Newbler contigs by GAM-NGS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Vicedomini, R., Vezzi, F., Scalabrin, S., Arvestad, L. &amp; Policriti, A. GAM-NGS: genomic assemblies merger for next generation sequencing. BMC Bioinformatics 7, S6 (2013)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d37551e777">15</a></sup> and 92,729 merged scaffolds were consequently generated. A total of 44,900 super-scaffolds were created by further scaffolding of the 92,729 sequences using SSPACE2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2010)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d37551e781">16</a></sup>, giving a total of 125.3 Gb MP reads. After excluding contaminated sequences and those shorter than 300 bases, the remaining 27,228 sequences were subjected to subsequent analysis as a draft genome sequence, TSUd_r1.0 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">SNP map and pseudomolecule construction</h3><p>A SNP linkage map was constructed with the F<sub>2</sub> population 92S80, generated from a cross between the subterranean clover cultivars Woogenellup and Daliak<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ghamkhar, K. et al. The first genetic maps for subterranean clover (Trifolium subterraneum L.) and comparative genomics with T. pratense L. and Medicago truncatula Gaertn. to identify new molecular markers for breeding. Mol Breed. 30, 213–226 (2011)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d37551e798">17</a></sup>. A total of 899,064 candidate SNPs were identified by mapping the 25.8 Gb Woogenellup and 337.9 Gb F<sub>2</sub> HiSeq 1000 PE reads onto the TSUd_r1.0 sequences and 52,635 SNPs were subsequently selected for Axiom<sup>®</sup> myDesign<sup>TM</sup> TG Array. Based on the genotypes of the 155 F<sub>2</sub> progenies obtained from the array, a SNP linkage map was constructed with 35,341 SNPs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S3</a> and <a href="http://clovergarden.jp/">http://clovergarden.jp/</a>). The mapped SNPs generated a total of 2,153 bins that were randomly located on eight linkage groups totaling 2,084 cM in length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep30358/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep30358/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep30358_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep30358/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep30358_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="645" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Anchoring the subterranean clover genome assembly to the genetic linkage map.</p><p>The 92S80 SNP linkage map (left bars) and 1,702 anchored TSUd_r1.1 scaffolds (right bars) with 35,341 SNPs. The crossbars on the linkage map show the position of SNP bins. The colors of bars represent numbers of mapped SNPs on each bin.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep30358/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A total of 1,505 TSUd_r1.0 scaffolds were anchored onto the linkage map through the mapped SNPs. Of the anchored scaffolds, 158 were located in multiple positions that suggested mis-assembly. Hence, the 158 scaffolds were split into 355 scaffolds according to the SNP positions on the linkage map. In addition, reverse complement sequences were created as revised scaffold sequences when the original sequences were anchored on the linkage map in a reverse direction. The modified TSUd_r1.0 sequences were designated as TSUd_r1.1. TSUd_r1.1 consisted of 27,424 scaffolds with 471,834,188 bp including 57,776,905 N bases. GC% and N50 were 33.3% and 287,605 bp, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S4</a>). The assembled sequences covered 85.4% of the subterranean clover genome (552.4 Mb), which was estimated based on kmer frequency distribution (kmer = 17, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S2</a>). Assembly quality of the TSUd_r1.1 sequences was investigated by mapping assembled sequences onto the 248 core eukaryotic genes (CEGs) by CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d37551e858">18</a></sup>. The numbers of ‘complete’ and ‘complete or partial’ mapped CEGs were 237 (95.6%) and 243 (98.0%), respectively, indicating high reliability of the assembled sequences.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the subterranean clover genome assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep30358/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The total number and length of anchored TSUd_r1.1 scaffolds on the SNP map were 1,702 and 384,208,136 bp (81.4% of TSUd_r1.1), respectively. Eight pseudomolecules, chr1-chr8, were constructed by connecting the anchored TSUd_r1.1 scaffolds with insertions of 10,000 N bases in between adjacent scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Total length of the eight pseudomolecules was 401.1 Mb including 54.9 Mb Ns. The length of each pseudomolecule ranged from 42.7 Mb (Chr7) to 63.7 Mb (Chr2, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Repetitive sequences</h3><p>Repetitive sequences in the assembled genome were identified by RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Proceedings of the 13 Annual International conference on Intelligent Systems for Molecular Biology (ISMB-05) Detroit, Michigan (2005)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1051">19</a></sup> and RepeatMasker. The total length of repetitive sequences in TSUd_r1.1 was 216.8 Mb, including 72.9 Mb known types and 143.9 Mb unique repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S5</a>). Of the known types of repeats, Class I LTR (Long Terminal Repeat) elements were observed most frequently. The ratios of repetitive sequences of the eight pseudomolecules ranged from 36.1% (chr7) to 46.0% Mb (chr6). A higher ratio of repetitive sequences was observed in chr0 (scaffolds that were not in pseudomolecules). Of the unique repeats observed on the eight pseudomolecules, 67.4Mb (62.8%) sequences were commonly observed in at least one of the other four legume species (red clover, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i> and common bean), while the other 39.8 Mb (37.2%) were subterranean clover-specific (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S6</a>). A total of 61,402 SSR sequences were identified in TSUd_r1.1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S7</a>). The average frequency of SSRs in overall and coding sequence (CDS) was 12.4 and 5.1 per 100 kb, respectively. The SSR frequency in CDS was lower than in the other four legume species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Gene prediction of TSUd_r1.1, employing MAKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Brandi, L. et al. MAKER: An easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1079">20</a></sup>, yielded a total 28,372 genes. Moreover, a further 14,334 genes were additionally predicted by Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19, Suppl. 2, ii215–ii225 (2003)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1083">21</a></sup>, giving a total of 42,706 predicted genes with average coding sequence length of 1,123 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S8</a>). In addition, 1,007 tRNA and 92 rRNA encoding genes were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S9</a>). Among the 42,706 putative genes, 37,085 (86.8%) were classified as non-TE genes whereas 5,621 (13.2%) were transposon elements (TEs), based on BLAST and domain searches against NCBI NR and InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Mulder, N. J. et al. New developments in the InterPro database. Nucl. Acids Res. 35 (Database issue), D224–D228 (2007)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1093">22</a></sup> databases, respectively. For comparisons with the gene sequences in other legume species, 36,800 subterranean clover putative non-TE genes were clustered with the genes predicted in red clover, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i> and common bean. This generated a total of 30,048 clusters. The number of subterranean clover-specific clusters was 14,745, whereas that commonly observed in genus <i>Trifolium</i> (subterranean and red clovers), tribe Trifolieae (clovers and <i>M. truncatula</i>) and clade Hologalegina (clovers, <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>L. japonicus</i>) was 1,906, 2,402 and 1,749, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). A total of 6,388 (21.3%) clusters were observed in all five legume species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep30358/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep30358/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep30358_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep30358/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep30358_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="675" height="413" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Number of subterranean clover protein encoding gene clusters with other legume species.</p><p>Subterranean clover: subterranean clover (S)-specific genes, Genus <i>Trifolium</i>: S- red clover (R) cluster, Tribe Trifolieae: S<i>-M. truncatula</i> (M) and S-R-M clusters, Hologalegina: S<i>-L. japonicus</i> (L), S-L-R, S-M-L and S-M-L-R, gene clusters, Others: S- common bean (C), S-C-R, S-M-C, S-L-C, S-R-M-C, S-R-L-C, S-M-L-C and S-R-M-L-C clusters.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep30358/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The 36,800 putative genes were further annotated using GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="The Gene Ontology Consortium. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25–29 (2000)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1155">23</a></sup>, KOG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Tatusov, R. L. et al. The COG database: an updated version includes eukaryotes, BMC Bioinformatics 4, 41 (2003)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1159">24</a></sup> and KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ogata, H. et al. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 29–34 (1999)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1163">25</a></sup> databases. A total of 30,543 (83.0%) genes were annotated with GO categories, including 10,328 genes involved in biological processes, 4,400 genes coding for cellular components and 15,815 genes associated with molecular function (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S4</a>). Of the 16,995 subterranean clover-specific genes (See <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>), only 6,449 (38%) genes were annotated while most genes (12,351, 88%) in the ‘Other’ cluster were classified in the GO categories. This result indicates that many of the subterranean clover-specific genes are novel with unknown function. Meanwhile, a total of 31,335 putative genes showed significant similarity to genes in the KOG database and 3,520 (11.2%), 6,004 (19.2%) and 5,165 (16.5%) genes were annotated in ‘information storage and processing’, ‘cellular processes and signaling’ and ‘metabolism’, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S5</a>). Genes belonging to the tribe Trifoliae cluster showed a higher ratio of ‘poorly characterized’ genes. The putative genes were also mapped onto a total of 1,688 enzyme encoding genes on KEGG pathways categorized ‘1. Metabolism’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Whole structure and variance in the genome of subterranean clover</h3><p>When the distribution of repetitive and putative gene sequences were surveyed at a whole genome scale, larger ratios of repetitive sequences and a smaller number of genes were observed in midsections of chr1, chr3, chr4, chr6, chr7 and chr8, suggesting heterochromatin regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Fig3">Fig. 3A</a>). However, candidate heterochromatin regions in chr2 and chr5 were insufficiently resolved to observe clearly. The ratios of common genes among the five legume species were lower in chr3, chr6 and chr8 than the other pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Fig3">Fig. 3B</a>). Higher ratios of subterranean clover-specific genes tended to be observed in euchromatin regions, with gene density being higher in chr1 and chr7 and lower in chr3 and chr6.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep30358/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep30358/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep30358_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep30358/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep30358_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="679" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Graphical view of the subterranean clover genome structure.</p><p>(<b>A</b>) Ratios of repetitive sequences in 1 Mb window. Red bars represent known repeats. Yellow bars show unique repeats commonly observed in the legume species, red clover<i>, M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i> and common bean, while green bars represent subterranean clover-specific sequences. The distance between horizontal lines shows 10% (<b>B</b>) Numbers of putative subterranean clover genes in a 1 Mb window. Red bars represents subterranean clover genes clustered with each of the other four legume species (red clover<i>, M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i> and common bean), yellow bars with three of the four species, green bars with two of the four species and blue bars with only one of the species, while orange bars show subterranean clover-specific genes. The distance between horizontal lines represents 20 genes. (<b>C</b>) CNV distribution. Green and red dots show log2 ratio plus and minus values, respectively. The distance between horizontal lines represent log2 ratio of 0.1.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep30358/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Copy number variations (CNVs) in the genomes of ‘Daliak’ and ‘Woogenellup’ were detected using the CNV-seq program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Xie, C. &amp; Tammi, M. T. CNV-seq, a new method to detect copy number variation using high-throughput sequencing. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 80 (2009)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1240">26</a></sup>. Higher log2 ratio (log-transformed ratio of the number of mapped reads in Woogenellup to the number in Daliak) were observed in the regions where densities of repetitive sequences were high (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Fig3">Fig. 3C</a>). The functions of the 35,341 SNPs mapped on the pseudomolecule on gene functions were predicted using SnpEff <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff: SNPs in the genome of Drosophila melanogaster strain w1118; iso-2; iso-3. Fly (Austin) 6, 80–92 (2012)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1247">27</a></sup>, which groups SNPs into four categories (high-impact, moderate, modifier and low), based on their positions in the genome sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S6</a>). Among 36,723 annotations on the 35,341 SNPs, 287 (0.8%) were classified as high-impact SNPs, including “splice acceptor and donor variants”, “loss of the start codon”, or “gain/loss of the stop codon”. Another 4,894 loci (13.3%) were classified as “moderate effects” (mis-sense variants), 26,107 (71.1%) as “modifiers” (e.g. variants in intergenic regions and introns) and 5,435 (14.8%) as “low-impact” (e.g. synonymous variants, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Table S11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Comparative analysis with other legume species</h3><p>A similarity search was performed, based on comparisons with the translated protein sequences of red clover, <i>M. truncatula, L. japonicus</i> and common bean (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Fig4">Fig. 4A</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S7</a>). Alignment of homologous sequence pairs along each pseudomolecule (Tsud_chr) revealed obvious syntenic relationships with chromosomes in <i>M. truncatula</i> (Mt_chr). Single synteny blocks were observed for three pseudomolecules (Tsud_chr1-<i>M. truncatula</i> chromosome 1(Mt_chr1), Tsud_chr3-Mt_chr3 and Tsud_chr5-Mt_chr5). Tsud_chr, 6, 7 and 8 shared two synteny blocks with Mt_chr3-chr6, Mt_chr3-chr6 and Mt_chr2-Chr8, respectively. The remaining two pseudomolecules shared three synteny blocks with Mt-chrs. Duplicated synteny blocks were observed in Tsud_chr2, chr4, chr5 and chr6. By contrast, clear synteny blocks were not shown between subterranean and red clovers. This might be caused by the shorter pseudomolecule (164.2 Mb of 309 Mb assembled genome) and larger ratio of unknown amino acid sequences in red clover (Percentages of X in translated protein sequences was 1.48% in red clover and 0.02% in subterranean clover). Nevertheless, possible synteny blocks were observed between Tsud_chr1-red clover linkage group 1 (Rc_LG1), Tsud_chr2-Rc_LG3, Tsud_chr5-Rc_LG2, Tsud_chr6-RC_LG2-LG4 and Tsud_chr7-RC_LG6-LG7. Clear synteny blocks were also observed between the subterranean clover genome and the other legume species. However, the blocks were more fragmented when compared with both <i>L. japonicus</i> and common bean, than when compared against <i>M. truncatula.</i> Overall, Tsud_chr1 is more conserved across the five legume species than the other pseudomolecules.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep30358/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep30358/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep30358_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep30358/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep30358_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="527" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Comparative analysis with other legume species.</p><p>(<b>A</b>) Graphical view of syntenic relationships between subterranean clover and <i>M</i>. <i>truncatula</i> (left) and between subterranean clover and red clover (right). Pink and blue dots show homologous sequences of Tsud_r1.1 with forward and reverse directions against the reference sequences. (<b>B</b>) Distribution of Ks values of orthologous gene pairs in subterranean clover and the four legume species. Subterranean clover, red clover, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i> and common bean are abbreviated to S, R, M, L and C, respectively. (<b>C</b>) A phylogenetic tree of 280 common single copy genes of the five legume species and <i>A. thaliana</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep30358/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Fifty-four percent of gene pairs between subterranean and red clovers showed a synonymous-substitution rate (<i>Ks</i> value) less than 0.1, suggesting a close relationship between the two clovers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Fig4">Fig. 4B</a>). A similar distribution of <i>Ks</i> value was observed between <i>M. truncatula</i> and the two clovers, although the basic chromosome numbers of the two clovers differ (subterranean clover n = 8, red clover n = 7). Interestingly, the peaks of <i>Ks</i> value between <i>L. japonicus</i> and common bean were almost the same as that between the three tribe Trifoleae species and <i>L. japonicus</i>.</p><p>To infer the divergence time among <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and the five legume species (common bean, <i>L. japonicus</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, red clover and subterranean clover), a phylogenetic tree was constructed based on 280 single copy genes commonly observed across the six species. According to the phylogenetic tree, it was estimated that the Hologalegina and Millettioid clades diverged ~54.8 MYA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#Fig4">Fig. 4C</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep30358#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. S8</a>), similar to the study of Lavin <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P. S. &amp; Wojciechowski, M. F. Evolutionary rates analysis of Leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst Biol. 54, 575–594 (2005)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1378">28</a></sup>. The divergence time between Robinioid (including <i>L japonicus</i>) and IRLC (Inverted Repeat Lacking Clade, including tribe Trifoliae) clades was ~42.7 MYA, while the genera <i>Trifolium</i> and <i>Medicago</i> diverged ~19.0 MYA. These two time estimations were more recent than the previous study of ~47.6 MYA and ~23 MYA, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="De Vega, J. J. et al. Red clover (Trifolium pratense L.) draft genome provides a platform for trait improvement. Sci. Rep. 5, 17394 (2015)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1391">9</a></sup>, which could be due to the different number of single copy genes used in the studies (280 in this manuscript and 818 in de Vega <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="De Vega, J. J. et al. Red clover (Trifolium pratense L.) draft genome provides a platform for trait improvement. Sci. Rep. 5, 17394 (2015)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1398">9</a></sup>). The divergence time between subterranean clover and red clover was estimated as 7.45 MYA.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>We constructed a 471.8 Mb subterranean clover draft genome, including eight pseudomolecules, totaling 401.1 Mb. This is the first draft genome of an annual forage clover. The assembled sequence covers 85.4% of the subterranean clover genome and is of high quality, according to CEGMA. Based on the 42,706 predicted genes, 14,745 subterranean clover-specific gene clusters were generated. These gene clusters will be a source for discovery of unique and potentially valuable genes in the species for a range of traits, including those leading to increased biomass production and persistence, disease and pest resistance, increased phosphorous-use efficiency and reduced methane emissions from grazing ruminant livestock<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Nichols, P. G. H. et al. Genetic improvement of subterranean clover (Trifolium subterraneum L.). 1. Germplasm, traits and future prospects. Crop Pasture Sci. 64, 312–346 (2013)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1411">2</a></sup>. Moreover, the high density linkage map, consisting of 35,341 SNPs and 287 high-impact SNPs annotated by SnpEff, will also greatly advance genetic and genomic analyses of subterranean clover.</p><p>Comparative analysis with the other legume species provided an insight into the evolution of Papilionoideae species. Synteny analysis revealed that the whole chromosome structure of chr1 in subterranean clover was highly conserved across the five Papilionoideae species compared and markedly more conserved than the other chromosomes. By contrast, duplicate synteny blocks were observed in subterranean clover chr2 against <i>M. truncatula</i> and red clover, suggesting that the genome duplication in chr2 occurred after the divergence of red and subterranean clovers ~7.45 MYA.</p><p>It is difficult to obtain high quality draft genomes in many of the most important forage legume species. The perennial species, red clover, white clover and alfalfa, show strong self-incompatibility, making it difficult to produce homozygous plants for genetic and genomic analysis. Even though a draft genome for red clover has been published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="De Vega, J. J. et al. Red clover (Trifolium pratense L.) draft genome provides a platform for trait improvement. Sci. Rep. 5, 17394 (2015)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1424">9</a></sup>, the quality of the subterranean clover genome in this study is much higher. For example, the percentages of complete and partial mapped CEGs by CEGMA in red clover were 85.5% and 95.6%, respectively, whereas those in subterranean clover were 95.6% and 98.0%. In addition, white clover and alfalfa are polyploid species. Subterranean clover is much better suited than these species for genomic and genetic studies because it is an annual, autogamous and diploid species. the results obtained in this study are expected to be a reference for genetic and genomic analyses within the <i>Trifolium</i> genus and also among other forage legumes. In the case of subterranean clover, a genome scaffold, a high resolution QTL map, the development of a world core collection and extensive phenotyping for important agro-morphological and economic traits have generated sufficient molecular genetic information to establish a comprehensive molecular breeding platform.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Materials and Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Materials and Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome sequencing and Assembly</h3><p>An Australian subterranean clover variety, cv. Daliak, was subjected to whole-genome shotgun sequencing using the Roche 454 GS FLX+, Illumina HiSeq 2000 and MiSeq platforms. Total cellular DNA was used for construction of a SE library for Roche 454 and PE libraries for Illumina HiSeq 2000 and MiSeq sequencing platforms according to the manufacturer’s instructions. A modified protocol proposed by Nieuwerburgh <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Nieuwerburgh, F. V. et al. Illumina mate-paired DNA sequencing-library preparation using Cre-Lox recombination. Nuc. Acids. Res. 40, e24 (2012)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1445">29</a></sup> was employed for MP library preparation. The obtained reads were subjected to quality control, as follows. Bases with quality scores less than 10 were filtered by PRINSEQ 0.20.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Schmieder, R. &amp; Edwards. R. Quality control and preprocessing of metagenomic datasets. Bioinformatics 27, 863–864 (2011)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1449">30</a></sup> and adaptor sequences in the reads were trimmed using fastx_clipper from the FASTX-Toolkit 0.0.13 (<a href="http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit">http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit</a>).</p><p>The 454 reads and OF reads created from MiSeq PE reads by COPE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Liu, B. et al. COPE: an accurate k-mer-based pair-end reads connection tool to facilitate genome assembly. Bioinformatics 28, 2870–2874 (2012)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1463">13</a></sup> were assembled using Newbler 2.7 (Roche Diagnostics, IN, USA). In parallel, HiSeq PE reads were assembled by SOAPdenovo2 (kmer = 61)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1467">14</a></sup> and the gaps closed by GapCloser 1.10 (p = 31) (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html</a>). The generated scaffolds were merged with the Newbler contigs by GAM-NGS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Vicedomini, R., Vezzi, F., Scalabrin, S., Arvestad, L. &amp; Policriti, A. GAM-NGS: genomic assemblies merger for next generation sequencing. BMC Bioinformatics 7, S6 (2013)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1478">15</a></sup>. The resultant sequences were further subjected to scaffolding with the MP reads using SSPACE2.0 with the parameters (-k 3 -x 0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2010)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1482">16</a></sup>. Potential contaminated sequences on the assembled scaffolds were identified and removed using BLASTN searches against the chloroplast genome sequence of <i>A. thaliana</i> (Accession number: NC_000932.1), mitochondrial genome sequence of <i>A. thaliana</i> (Accession number: NC_001284.2), bacterial genome sequences registered in NCBI (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov</a>) and vector sequences from UniVec (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/tools/vecscreen/univec/">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/tools/vecscreen/univec/</a>) with E-value cutoff of 1E-10 and length coverage ≥10%. Assembly quality was investigated by CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1507">18</a></sup>. Repetitive sequences were searched using RepeatScount 1.0.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Proceedings of the 13 Annual International conference on Intelligent Systems for Molecular Biology (ISMB-05) Detroit, Michigan (2005)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1511">19</a></sup> and RepeatMasker 3.30 (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>). SSR motifs were identified using the SciRoKo software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Kofler, R., Schlötterer, C. &amp; Lelley, T. SciRoKo: A new tool for whole genome icrosatellite search and investigation. Bioinformatics 23, 1683–1685 (2007)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1523">31</a></sup> in the “MISA” mode with default parameters. The genome size of subterranean clover was estimated by kmer frequency distribution (kmer = 17) using Jellyfish ver. 2.1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Marçais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1527">32</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">SNP map and pseudomolecule construction</h3><p>A total of 155 F<sub>2</sub> derived F<sub>4</sub> bulked DNAs of the 92S80 population were used for SNP linkage map construction. The trimmed Illumina SE and PE reads of the Woogenellup parent (DRR018261, DRR018262, DRR024180 and DRR024181) were mapped onto TSUd_r1.0 using Bowtie 2 2.2.34 (parameters maxins = 1000)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1543">33</a></sup>. SNP candidates were called based on the mapping result using samtools mpileup ver.1.1.19 (parameters: –Duf –d 1000000)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence alignment/map (SAM) format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1547">34</a></sup> and subsequently filtered using VCFtools ver 1.1.19<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Danecek, P., Auton, A. &amp; Abecasis, G. The Variant Call Format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics. 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1551">35</a></sup>. An Axiom<sup>®</sup> myDesign<sup>TM</sup> TG Array was designed for a total of 59,105 SNPs, which exhibited Minor allele frequency ≥30 and Missing data ≤30%. The linkage map was constructed using MultiPoint 3.3 (<a href="http://www.multiqtl.com/">http://www.multiqtl.com/</a>) with the following parameters: Population type = F2, Min LOD threshold = 10.0, Max threshold rf = 0.25, Kosambi mapping function.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>tRNA genes were predicted using tRNAscan-SE ver. 1.23<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucl. Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1575">36</a></sup> with default parameters. rRNA genes were predicted by BLAST searches with an E-value cutoff of 1E-10. The <i>A. thaliana</i> 5.8S and 25S rRNAs (accession number: X52320.1) and 18S rRNA (accession number: X16077.1) were used as query sequences.</p><p>Protein-encoding sequences in the assembled genomic sequences were predicted by MAKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Brandi, L. et al. MAKER: An easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1585">20</a></sup>. Published SRA transcript sequences of red clover (SRX351919, SRX351918, SRX351917, SRX351791) and white clover (ERX324290) were assembled by Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Grabherr, N. G. et al. Trinity: reconstructing a full-length transcriptome without a genome from RNA-Seq data. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1589">37</a></sup> for evidence-based gene prediction. The assembled 431,700 red clover and 217,133 white clover unigenes were mapped onto the subterranean pseudomolecules by BLAT (-t = dnax –q = dnax minIdentity = 25), together with the 62,319 <i>M. truncatula</i> CDS (4.0 v1). In parallel, <i>Ab initio</i> gene prediction was performed by Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19, Suppl. 2, ii215–ii225 (2003)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1599">21</a></sup> using the <i>A. thaliana</i> training set. The two sets of predicted gene sequences were merged by MAKER. Genes related to transposable elements (TEs) were detected by BLASTP searches against the NCBI non-redundant (nr) protein database (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov</a>) with an E-value cutoff of 1E-10 and InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier, Nucleic Acids Res., 33, W116–W120 (2005)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1614">38</a></sup> searches against the InterPro database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Mulder, N. J. et al. New developments in the InterPro database. Nucl. Acids Res. 35 (Database issue), D224–D228 (2007)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1618">22</a></sup> with an E-value cutoff of 1.0. The putative non-TE genes were classified into the plant gene ontology (GO) slim categories<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="The Gene Ontology Consortium. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25–29 (2000)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1622">23</a></sup> and the “euKaryotic clusters of Orthologous Groups” (KOG) categories<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Tatusov, R. L. et al. The COG database: an updated version includes eukaryotes, BMC Bioinformatics 4, 41 (2003)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1626">24</a></sup> and then mapped onto the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) reference pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ogata, H. et al. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 29–34 (1999)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1631">25</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">SNP annotation and CNV analysis</h3><p>CNVs were analyzed using CNV-seq ver. 0.2.7 (parameters: –genome-size 239,146,348)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Xie, C. &amp; Tammi, M. T. CNV-seq, a new method to detect copy number variation using high-throughput sequencing. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 80 (2009)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1643">26</a></sup>. SNP effects on gene function in TSUd_r1.1 were predicted using SnpEff ver. 4.0 g (parameters: –no-downstream, -no-upstream)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff: SNPs in the genome of Drosophila melanogaster strain w1118; iso-2; iso-3. Fly (Austin) 6, 80–92 (2012)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1647">27</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Comparative analysis</h3><p>Translated protein sequences of subterranean clover were clustered by using the CD-HIT program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Fu, L,, Niu, B., Zhu, Z., Wu, S and Li, W. CD-HIT: accelerated for clustering the next-generation sequencing data. Bioinformatics 28, 3150-3152 (2012)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1660">39</a></sup> with those of common bean (v1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Schmutz, J. et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat. Genet. 46, 707–713 (2014)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1664">5</a></sup>, <i>L. japonicus</i> (rel.3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome Structure of the Legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227–239 (2008)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1671">10</a></sup>, <i>M. truncatula</i> (4.0 v1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1679">11</a></sup> and red clover (v2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="De Vega, J. J. et al. Red clover (Trifolium pratense L.) draft genome provides a platform for trait improvement. Sci. Rep. 5, 17394 (2015)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1683">9</a></sup> with the parameters c = 0.6 and aL = 0.5. Homologous translated proteins sequences were searched by BLAST searches with an E-value cut-off of 1E-100 and the synteny plot was made using the gnuplot program (<a href="http://www.gnuplot.info">http://www.gnuplot.info</a>). <i>Ks</i> value was estimated for the gene pairs with reciprocal best hits of BLAST searches with an E-value cut-off of 1E-10, using KaKs Calculator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang, Z., Li, J., Zhao, X. Q., Wang, J., Wong, G. K. &amp; Yu, J. KaKs Calculator: Calculating Ka and Ks through model selection and model averaging. Genomics Proteomics Bioinformatics 4, 259–263 (2006)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1697">40</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>CD-HIT analysis against the six species (<i>A. thaliana</i> (TAIR10), common bean (v1.0), <i>L. japonicus</i> (rel.3.0), <i>M. truncatula</i> (4.0 v1), red clover (v2.1) and subterranean clover (TSUd_r1.0)) were conducted with the parameters c = 0.6 and aL = 0.9. The single copy genes in each cluster commonly conserved among the six species were applied to multiple alignment by MUSCLE 3.8.31<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: a multiple sequence alignment method with reduced time and space complexity. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 113 (2004)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1718">41</a></sup>. The indels in the aligned sequences of the single copy genes were eliminated by Gblocks 0.91b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Castresana, J. Selection of conserved blocks from multiple alignments for their use in phylogenetic analysis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 540–552 (2000)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1722">42</a></sup>. The sequences of conserved blocks in the single copy genes were concatenated for each species and were used for construction of a phylogenetic tree by the Maximum-Likelihood (ML) algorithm, using MEGA 7.0.9 beta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis Version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725–2729 (2013)." href="/articles/srep30358#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d37551e1727">43</a></sup> with the Jones-Taylor-Thornton (JJT) model as substitution model. The time values were calculated according to the distances in the phylogenetic tree constructed by Maximum Likelihood method. The uniform rates defined in the MEGA program were used for converting the distances to time values. In this step, <i>A. thaliana</i> was selected as outgroup. According to TIMETREE (<a href="http://www.timetree.org">http://www.timetree.org</a>), the divergence time between <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>M. truncatula</i> is 114.0 MYA and this value was used for calibration. The divergence times among the six species were calculated by using MEGA 7.0.9 beta.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Data availability</h3><p>The genome assembly data (scaffolds and pseudomolecule), annotations, gene models and SNPs on the linkage map are available at the Clover GARDEN (<a href="http://clovergarden.jp/">http://clovergarden.jp/</a>). The genome sequence reads obtained are available from the DDBJ Sequence Read Archive (DRA) under the accession numbers DRA003274. The BioProject accession number of the study is PRJDB2012. The WGS and CON accession numbers of assembled sequences are BCLP01000001-BCLP01066167 (66,167 entries) and DF973112-DF976994 (3,883 entries). The protein IDs are GAU09980.1-GAU52038.1.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec18" data-title="Additional Information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec18-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec18">Additional Information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec18-content"><p><b>How to cite this article</b>: Hirakawa, H. <i>et al.</i> Draft genome sequence of subterranean clover, a reference for genus <i>Trifolium</i>. <i>Sci. Rep.</i> <b>6</b>, 30358; doi: 10.1038/srep30358 (2016).</p></div></div></section>
